src=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/627240/fpls-12-627240-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-627240-g005.jpg\" alt=\"www.frontiersin.org\" id=\"F5\" loading=\"lazy\">\n  \u003C/picture>\n\u003C/a>\r\n\u003Cp>\u003Cstrong>Figure 5.\u003C/strong> Developmental pathways observed in \u003Ci>C. sativa\u003C/i> (industrial hemp) microspore culture. \u003Cb>(A&#x2013;C)\u003C/b> Male gametophyte development in \u003Ci>C. sativa\u003C/i> during \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> culture. \u003Cb>(A)\u003C/b> Uninucleate microspores; \u003Cb>(B)\u003C/b> uninucleate microspores after 3 days in culture media; \u003Cb>(C)\u003C/b> symmetrically divided microspore with two equally sized nuclei; \u003Cb>(D)\u003C/b> multinucleate structure without organization and still enclosed in exine; \u003Cb>(E)\u003C/b> globular multicellular structure with developing exine; and \u003Cb>(F)\u003C/b> heart-shape embryo with two distinct domains. The nuclei in \u003Cb>(A&#x2013;C)\u003C/b> are stained with the nuclear dye 4&#x2032;,6-diamidino-2-phenylindole (DAPI) to indicate viability.\u003C/p>\r\n\u003C/div>\r\n\u003Cdiv class=\"clear\">\u003C/div>\r\n\u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\">\u003C/div>\r\n\u003Ch4>\u003Ci>In vitro\u003C/i> Mutagenesis\u003C/h4>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">A mutation occurs in DNA, naturally or it can also be induced artificially. The majority of the genetic variation existing in a gene pool has occurred naturally. These genetic variations can be recombined through conventional breeding practices to develop a novel variety with desired gene traits. Although these spontaneous mutations are frequent, the desired mutation in the desired gene segment altering its biological role is extremely rare. Therefore, mutation induction tools are used in the rapid development of genetic variability in crops. For the last few decades, there were several scientific reports published assessing the impact of an induced mutation in the improvement of crops (\u003Ca href=\"#B17\">Brock, 1971\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B18\">Broertjes and Van Harten, 1988\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B117\">Micke, 1999\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B131\">Oladosu et al., 2016\u003C/a>). However, in cannabis research and development is rapidly flourishing, but there are only a few reports on targeted mutation through genetic transformation (\u003Ca href=\"#B50\">Feeney and Punja, 2003\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B163\">Slusarkiewicz-Jarzina et al., 2005\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B161\">Sirkowski, 2012\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B185\">Wahby et al., 2013\u003C/a>) and there is no mutant variety introduced at the commercial level. \u003Ci>In vitro\u003C/i> culture techniques, coupled with mutagenesis, has simplified the crop improvement work for both seeds and vegetatively propagated plants (\u003Ca href=\"#B79\">Hussain et al., 2012\u003C/a>). Little efforts have been made and published to establish DH production in cannabis, but once streamlined will open up exciting opportunities for DH mutagenesis as it has been successfully employed in canola (\u003Ca href=\"#B175\">Szarejko, 2003\u003C/a>).\u003C/p>\r\n\u003Ch3>Synthetic Seed Technology\u003C/h3>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">Synthetic seeds usually refer to artificially encapsulated somatic embryos (\u003Ca href=\"#B124\">Murashige, 1977\u003C/a>) but have also been used in reference to encapsulated vegetative tissues that have the potential to develop into a whole plant (auxiliary buds, cell aggregates, shoot buds). Somatic embryos provide the ideal approach to developing synthetic seeds as they often have the ability to survive desiccation and can be treated in much the same way as true seeds. At the same time, other tissues lack this capacity and are less useful (\u003Ca href=\"#B147\">Rihan et al., 2017\u003C/a>). As shown in \u003Ca href=\"#F6\">Figure 6\u003C/a>, synthetic seeds can be successfully developed by using various explants, media, and encapsulation protocols (\u003Ca href=\"#B11\">Bapat et al., 1987\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B35\">Corrie and Tandon, 1993\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B130\">Nyende et al., 2003\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B24\">Chand and Singh, 2004\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B143\">Rai et al., 2008\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B96\">Lata et al., 2009a\u003C/a>).\u003C/p>\r\n\u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\">\u003C/div>\r\n\u003Cdiv class=\"Imageheaders\">FIGURE 6\u003C/div>\r\n\u003Cdiv class=\"FigureDesc\">\r\n\u003Ca href=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/627240/fpls-12-627240-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-627240-g006.jpg\" name=\"figure6\" target=\"_blank\">\r\n\n  \u003Cpicture>\n    \u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=480&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/627240/fpls-12-627240-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-627240-g006.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 563px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=370&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/627240/fpls-12-627240-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-627240-g006.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 1024px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=290&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/627240/fpls-12-627240-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-627240-g006.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 1441px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=410&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/627240/fpls-12-627240-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-627240-g006.jpg\" media=\"\">\u003Csource type=\"image/jpg\" srcset=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/627240/fpls-12-627240-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-627240-g006.jpg\" media=\"\"> \u003Cimg src=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/627240/fpls-12-627240-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-627240-g006.jpg\" alt=\"www.frontiersin.org\" id=\"F6\" loading=\"lazy\">\n  \u003C/picture>\n\u003C/a>\r\n\u003Cp>\u003Cstrong>Figure 6.\u003C/strong> General schematic diagram showing steps for calcium chloride encapsulated synthetic seed production.\u003C/p>\r\n\u003C/div>\r\n\u003Cdiv class=\"clear\">\u003C/div>\r\n\u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\">\u003C/div>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb15 w100pc float_left mt15\">Cannabis is generally a cross-pollinating crop, and due to its allogamous nature, it is difficult to maintain existing elite varieties by seed. Typically, a minimum isolation distance of 5 km between breeding nurseries and hemp production fields is required to minimize the occurrence of nuisance pollen. Such separation is often difficult to achieve in areas with high hemp production intensity. Therefore, \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> propagation using synthetic seed technology is an alternative method for large-scale clonal propagation and germplasm preservation. As the cannabis industry grows, this method may be cheaper and faster than traditional tissue culture methods. Along with the preservation of genetic uniformity, clones produced through this technique are pathogen-free, easy to handle, and transport.\u003C/p>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb15\">Moreover, in other species, this approach has resulted in increased quality of planting material (\u003Ca href=\"#B147\">Rihan et al., 2017\u003C/a>). While cannabis tissue culture methods are still being optimized, \u003Ca href=\"#B96\">Lata et al. (2009a)\u003C/a> developed a high-frequency propagation of axillary buds of \u003Ci>C. sativa\u003C/i> encapsulated in calcium alginate gel. Calcium alginate is a hydrogel that contains nutrients, growth regulators, and sometimes antibiotics.\u003C/p>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb15\">When directly sown on a substrate, encapsulation aids in the physical protection and establishment and growth of the explant. According to \u003Ca href=\"#B96\">Lata et al. (2009a)\u003C/a>, gel capsule consisted of 5% sodium alginate with 50 mM CaCl\u003Csub>2\u003C/sub>.2H\u003Csub>2\u003C/sub>O, and full-strength MS medium supplied with 0.5 &#x03BC;M TDZ, and 0.075% plant preservative mixture (PPM). The optimal regrowth and conversion were achieved in MS medium supplemented with antimicrobial components, PPM (0.075%) and TDZ (0.5 &#x03BC;M) under \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> conditions. Under \u003Ci>in vivo\u003C/i> condition, the optimal conversion and regrowth were exhibited on 1:1 potting mix-fertilome with coco natural growth medium supplied with MS medium containing 3% sucrose, 0.5% PPM. Clones regenerated from the explants were successfully hardened and transferred to the soil (\u003Ca href=\"#B96\">Lata et al., 2009a\u003C/a>).\u003C/p>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">Another hurdle to \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> propagation is transporting requested strains from the tissue culture facility to the growers in a timely manner. These transportation issues become incredibly challenging for maintaining crop schedules because cannabis crops can take more than 2 months to reach hardening stages, then spend 4 weeks in vegetative growth, then 7 or 8 weeks in flower. Greenhouse or indoor growers require a consistent supply demand to receive a high volume of plantlets every week to start over a new grow room at a very tight on-time delivery schedule, which is the most important metric in their operations. An established and cost-effective synthetic seed encapsulation technique would provide an opportunity to eliminate the transportation challenge.\u003C/p>\r\n\u003Ca id=\"h6\" name=\"h6\">\u003C/a>\u003Ch2>Cryopreservation\u003C/h2>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb15\">Cryopreservation refers to the storage of diverse living materials at below &#x2013;130&#x00B0;C (\u003Ca href=\"#B44\">Engelmann, 2004\u003C/a>). It serves as an alternative conservation approach to the conventional field and \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> (i.e., slow growth) germplasm conservation and is cost-effective over extended periods with minimal space and routine maintenance requirements (\u003Ca href=\"#B135\">Pence, 2011\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B45\">Engelmann, 2014\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B141\">Popova et al., 2015\u003C/a>). It also assists current breeding programs by providing long-term storage and an easy long-distance exchange of genetic materials (e.g., pollen and meristematic apices and buds). Cryopreservation has been implemented for various plant species using different methods, the most popular and widely applicable, including controlled freezing, vitrification, encapsulation-dehydration, encapsulation-vitrification, and droplet-vitrification (\u003Ca href=\"#B150\">Sakai and Engelmann, 2007\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B141\">Popova et al., 2015\u003C/a>). These methods follow distinct approaches to dehydrate cryopreserving living materials by converting liquid water to a glassy state to avoid the lethal formation of intracellular ice. The selection of methods and the scales of conservation using this approach are strongly determined by genotypes and tissue materials used, which contain different responses to pre- and post-cryopreservation treatments.\u003C/p>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">Conventional and \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> conservation of cannabis require considerable amounts of space and routine maintenance, have genetic mutations accumulate in the plants. Conventional conservation may expose plants to virulence pathogens. The plants may eventually become susceptible to diseases. The application of cryopreservation can serve as an essential tool for the conservation of various valuable \u003Ci>C. sativa\u003C/i> genotypes with unique attributes and trading the genotypes nationally and internationally in sterile conditions. The first study on applying cryopreservation techniques in \u003Ci>C. sativa\u003C/i> was reported in 1989 using cell suspension cultures (\u003Ca href=\"#B84\">Jekkel et al., 1989\u003C/a>). The suspension cultures were preserved using 10% dimethyl sulfoxide (DMSO) cryoprotectant and a controlled cooling rate of 2&#x00B0;C/min and transfer temperature of &#x2013;10&#x00B0;C, with a 58% survival rate after cryopreservation of the cultures. A cryopreservation protocol for \u003Ci>C. sativa\u003C/i> shoot tips was recently developed using a droplet-vitrification in liquid nitrogen for long-term conservation of this crop (\u003Ca href=\"#B182\">Uchendu et al., 2019\u003C/a>). The report showed that vitrified shoot tips using a cryoprotectant solution of 30% glycerol, 15% ethylene glycol, 15% DMSO in liquid MS medium with 0.4 M sucrose, pH 5.8 had 63% re-growth efficiency. Despite the promising progress made, more studies need to be done on selecting appropriate cryopreservation methods with respect to the tissue types and genotypes, increasing re-growth and survival efficiency of preserved samples, and genetic stability of regenerated plants after using different cryopreservation tools, among others.\u003C/p>\r\n\u003Ca id=\"h7\" name=\"h7\">\u003C/a>\u003Ch2>Germplasm Maintenance\u003C/h2>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">The \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> condition also raises some issues for concern, primarily when the material is maintained over a long period of time.\u003C/p>\r\n\u003Ch3>Clonal Stability \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> Culture\u003C/h3>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">\u003Ci>In vitro\u003C/i> mass-propagation and maintenance of elite germplasm requires genetically stable true-to-type clones. Several factors, such as the number of subcultures, changes in the relationship of auxin/cytokinin, explant type, and a high concentration of growth regulators, may influence the genetic stability of a clone under \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> conditions (\u003Ca href=\"#B89\">Joyce et al., 2003\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B152\">Sato et al., 2011\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B169\">Smulders and de Klerk, 2011\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B129\">Nwauzoma and Jaja, 2013\u003C/a>). While carefully selecting explant types and optimizing the conditions above, but depending on the plant species, clonal stability can be obtained during \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> mass-propagation and germplasm conservation of the desired elite genotypes maintained. To date, \u003Ci>C. sativa\u003C/i> plants regenerated from nodal culture, and \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> conserved synthetic seeds (&#x2018;Encapsulated&#x2019; nodal segments) have shown no evidence of genetic mutations; however, this has only been evaluated using low numbers of markers (\u003Ca href=\"#B98\">Lata et al., 2010a\u003C/a>, \u003Ca href=\"#B101\">2011\u003C/a>). Despite optimizing and using properly \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> conditions that limit somaclonal variations, assessment of clonal stability is required to ensure the regenerated clones are the true-to-type of the donor plants.\u003C/p>\r\n\u003Ch3>Somaclonal Variation\u003C/h3>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb15\">Although clonal propagation and maintenance of elite germplasm require a substantial genetic uniformity among \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> regenerated plantlets, there may be a large possibility of genetic variations, called &#x201C;somaclonal variation&#x201D; among these plants and/or relative to the donor plants. Somaclonal variation is commonly a result of genetic alterations and changes in the new \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> plants&#x2019; epigenetics compared to the original source plants (\u003Ca href=\"#B118\">Miguel and Marum, 2011\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B1\">Abreu et al., 2014\u003C/a>). The frequency and nature of somaclonal variation \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> culture can be influenced by different factors, such as explant source, genotype, \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> techniques, \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> growth conditions, length of the culture period, and the number of subcultures. The use of \u003Ci>de novo\u003C/i> regeneration from highly differentiated tissues (i.e., roots, leaves, stems, hypocotyls, cotyledons, etc.) is generally considered to produce more somaclonal variation compared to explants with developed meristems (i.e., axillary buds and shoot tips) (\u003Ca href=\"#B136\">Pijut et al., 2012\u003C/a>). Most of these factors generate oxidative stress during culture initiation and subsequent subculturing. The explants and the subsequent regenerated plants exposed to the stress may retain genetic changes. For example, protoplast and callus based plant regeneration impose a high degree of oxidative stress; thus, the stress promotes a high mutation rate, whereas plants regenerated through auxiliary branching (e.g., nodes, shoot tip) experience very low oxidative stress, normally resulting in no genetic variation (\u003Ca href=\"#B197\">Zayova et al., 2010\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B169\">Smulders and de Klerk, 2011\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B94\">Krishna et al., 2016\u003C/a>). Genetic variation can also arise from somatic mutations already present in the explants collected from the donor plant (\u003Ca href=\"#B90\">Karp, 1994\u003C/a>). \u003Ci>In vitro\u003C/i> regeneration of plants can also be genotype-specific, in which genotypes have different degrees of mutation risks and thus strongly determine the formation of somaclonal variation (\u003Ca href=\"#B7\">Alizadeh et al., 2010\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B42\">Eftekhari et al., 2012\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B129\">Nwauzoma and Jaja, 2013\u003C/a>). The genetic alterations strongly depend on the \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> techniques used to regenerate \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> plants. Additionally, despite differences across plant species, cultures maintained for a long period tend to generate high somaclonal variation, and \u003Ci>vice versa\u003C/i> (\u003Ca href=\"#B49\">Farahani et al., 2011\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B85\">Jevremovic et al., 2012\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B174\">Sun et al., 2013\u003C/a>). When cultures are getting old and continuously subcultured, the chance of generating genetically less uniform plants is increased (\u003Ca href=\"#B197\">Zayova et al., 2010\u003C/a>), but depends upon the plant species. For example, any more than eight subculture cycles increased somaclonal variation in banana (\u003Ca href=\"#B91\">Khan et al., 2011\u003C/a>), whereas over 30 subcultures did not cause any detectable somaclonal variations in \u003Ci>C. sativa\u003C/i> (\u003Ca href=\"#B98\">Lata et al., 2010a\u003C/a>).\u003C/p>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb15\">Although the molecular mechanism of how somaclonal variations generated from a single plant genotype under the same \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> conditions is not fully explored, several potential mechanisms causing genetic alternations and epigenetics have been proposed in different plant species. These mechanisms include changes in chromosome number, point mutations, somatic crossing over and sister chromatid exchange, chromosome breakage and rearrangement, somatic gene rearrangement, DNA replication, changes in organelle DNA, insertion or excision of transposable elements, segregation of pre-existing chimeral tissues, DNA methylation, epigenetic variation, and histone modifications and RNA interference (\u003Ca href=\"#B152\">Sato et al., 2011\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B94\">Krishna et al., 2016\u003C/a>; references therein).\u003C/p>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">The occurrence of somaclonal variations in regenerated \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> plants may be advantageous or disadvantageous, depending on \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> propagation goals. If \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> propagation aims to generate new variants, obtaining variations among \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> plants can be advantageous that increases genetic diversity for a genotype used. It provides an alternative tool to the breeders for obtaining genetic variability in different plant species, which are either difficult to breed or have narrow genetic bases. On the flip side, when \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> propagation targets to produce multiple true-to-type \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> plants and maintain elite germplasm, the occurrence of subtle somaclonal variations is a severe problem.\u003C/p>\r\n\u003Ca id=\"h8\" name=\"h8\">\u003C/a>\u003Ch2>Phytocannabinoid Synthesis in the Cannabis Species\u003C/h2>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">Nature has deftly adorned cannabis species with a spectrum of phytocannabinoids or monoterpenoids that are chemically designed with para-oriented isoprenyl and aralkyl groups (\u003Ca href=\"#B71\">Hanus et al., 2016\u003C/a>). Since the discovery of tetrahydrocannabinol (THC) and cannabidiol (CBD) in the early 1960s, there are over 120 cannabinoids that has been reported, and the biosynthesis pathway of these compounds has been greatly improved (\u003Ca href=\"#B176\">Taura et al., 1995\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B160\">Sirikantaramas et al., 2004\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B178\">Taura et al., 2007b\u003C/a>, \u003Ca href=\"#B179\">2009\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B58\">Gagne et al., 2012\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B173\">Stout et al., 2012\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B103\">Laverty et al., 2019\u003C/a>). Presumably, cannabigerolic acid (CBGA), the product formed by the alkylation of geranyl diphosphate and olivetol, is the key precursor compound in the synthesis of cannabinoids (\u003Ca href=\"#B51\">Fellermeier and Zenk, 1998\u003C/a>). The cyclization event of prenyl components of CBGA, catalyzed by three enzymes &#x2013; tetrahydrocannabinolic acid synthase (THCAS) (genebank accession: AB057805), cannabidiolic acid synthase (CBDAS) (genebank accession: AB292682), and cannabichromenic acid synthase (CBCAS), lead to the formation of three major cannabinoids, THCA, CBDA, and CBCA, respectively (\u003Ca href=\"#B160\">Sirikantaramas et al., 2004\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B177\">Taura et al., 2007a\u003C/a>). Biochemical characterization of the enzymes, THCAS and CBDAS, have demonstrated that the enzymes follow a similar reaction mechanism. In the presence of molecular oxygen, the enzymes use flavin adenine dinucleotide (FAD) cofactor to catalyze CBGA forming THCA and CBDA, and hydrogen peroxide as its chemical biproduct (\u003Ca href=\"#B160\">Sirikantaramas et al., 2004\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B178\">Taura et al., 2007b\u003C/a>). Although it is a bit unclear, the chemical reaction for CBCAS also believed to use FAD as cofactor and molecular oxygen to complete the enzymatic activity on CBGA. The genes that encode for CBCAS and THCAS are highly similar in the nucleotide level, indicating that CBCAS is also flavoproteins, like the other two enzymes, requiring oxygen to catalyze CBGA to CBCA (\u003Ca href=\"#B103\">Laverty et al., 2019\u003C/a>). THCA, CBDA, and CBCA are the major cannabinoids in acidic forms that are synthesized in cannabis plant; upon decarboxylation, these compounds convert into neutral forms, THC, CBD, and CBC respectively (\u003Ca href=\"#B187\">Wang et al., 2016\u003C/a>).\u003C/p>\r\n\u003Ca id=\"h9\" name=\"h9\">\u003C/a>\u003Ch2>Determination of Genetic Fidelity\u003C/h2>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">Variations between regenerated and donor plants can be exhibited at phenotypic, cytological, biochemical, and genetic/epigenetic levels (\u003Ca href=\"#B77\">Hillig, 2005\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B118\">Miguel and Marum, 2011\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B169\">Smulders and de Klerk, 2011\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B1\">Abreu et al., 2014\u003C/a>). These variations can be determined through different approaches, such as morphological, cytological, biochemical, and molecular analyses (\u003Ca href=\"#F7\">Figure 7\u003C/a>). For morphological traits, changes are not always observed at early developmental stages or may not entirely display the variations. By contrast, the use of cytological and molecular detection approaches determines differences at chromosomal and DNA levels, respectively, regardless of the developmental stages in various plant species (\u003Ca href=\"#B31\">Clarindo et al., 2012\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B134\">Pathak and Dhawan, 2012\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B37\">Currais et al., 2013\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B1\">Abreu et al., 2014\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B13\">Bello-Bello et al., 2014\u003C/a>). To date, several studies have been reported on the use of different molecular markers in \u003Ci>Cannabis\u003C/i> spp. genetic diversity, fingerprinting, etc. These markers include random amplified polymorphic DNA (RAPD), restriction fragment length polymorphisms (RFLP), amplified fragment length polymorphism (AFLP), microsatellites, inter simple sequence repeat (ISSR), short tandem repeat (STR) multiplex, and single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) and PCR Allele Competitive Extension (PACE) assay (\u003Ca href=\"#B46\">Faeti et al., 1996\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B93\">Kojoma et al., 2002\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B5\">Alghanim and Almirall, 2003\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B64\">Gilmore and Peakall, 2003\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B70\">Hakki et al., 2003\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B38\">Datwyler and Weiblen, 2006\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B116\">Mendoza et al., 2009\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B98\">Lata et al., 2010a\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B61\">Gao et al., 2014\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B41\">Dufresnes et al., 2017\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B75\">Henry et al., 2018\u003C/a>). These molecular markers coupled with cytological and morphological analyses (\u003Ca href=\"#B1\">Abreu et al., 2014\u003C/a>) are valuable techniques to ensure the genetic stability of \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> regenerated plants or \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> conserved germplasm of \u003Ci>C. sativa\u003C/i>. To date, only ISSR markers have been used to confirm the genetic stability of \u003Ci>C. sativa\u003C/i> synthetic seeds during \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> multiplication and storage for 6 months under different growth conditions, and \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> propagated plants over 30 nodal subcultures in culture and hardening in soil for 8 months, compared to the corresponding donor plants (\u003Ca href=\"#B98\">Lata et al., 2010a\u003C/a>, \u003Ca href=\"#B101\">2011\u003C/a>).\u003C/p>\r\n\u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\">\u003C/div>\r\n\u003Cdiv class=\"Imageheaders\">FIGURE 7\u003C/div>\r\n\u003Cdiv class=\"FigureDesc\">\r\n\u003Ca href=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/627240/fpls-12-627240-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-627240-g007.jpg\" name=\"figure7\" target=\"_blank\">\r\n\n  \u003Cpicture>\n    \u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=480&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/627240/fpls-12-627240-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-627240-g007.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 563px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=370&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/627240/fpls-12-627240-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-627240-g007.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 1024px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=290&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/627240/fpls-12-627240-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-627240-g007.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 1441px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=410&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/627240/fpls-12-627240-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-627240-g007.jpg\" media=\"\">\u003Csource src=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/627240/fpls-12-627240-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-627240-g007.jpg\" alt=\"www.frontiersin.org\" id=\"F7\" loading=\"lazy\">\n  \u003C/picture>\n\u003C/a>\r\n\u003Cp>\u003Cstrong>Figure 7.\u003C/strong> A flow chart depicting different approaches that can be used to determine the genetic stability of \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> regenerated or conserved cannabis plants, compared to its donor counterparts.\u003C/p>\r\n\u003C/div>\r\n\u003Cdiv class=\"clear\">\u003C/div>\r\n\u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\">\u003C/div>\r\n\u003Ca id=\"S9\" name=\"S9\">\u003C/a>\u003Ch2>Projected Contribution of Tissue Culture in the Global Cannabis Industry\u003C/h2>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">The present global cannabis market is worth &#x0024;340 B\u003Csup id=\"footnotesuper2\">\u003Ca id=\"footnote2a\">\u003C/a>\u003Ca class=\"footnoteanchor\" href=\"#footnote2\">2\u003C/a>\u003C/sup>. To supply cannabis (medical and recreational) to global consumers, a stable supply chain of quality production and value-added product development still needs to be established. Considering the average annual weighted usage base of 110 g per customer (Canaccord Genuity), the global cannabis demand currently could be around 19-20 M kg per year. Major cannabis consumers are in Europe, North America, South America, Asia, and Oceanic parts of the world, with an estimate of 263 million people using the drug in the previous year (European Consumer Stables Report, 2018; World Drug Report, 2019). To produce 20 M kg of cannabis every year, considering a 40-gm yield per plant, would require 500 M clones/seeds a year. An average price of &#x0024;10, as, then, the overall present global expected market size for tissue culture clones/manual clones could be predicted around &#x0024;5B. With intensive indoor cultivation, tissue culture clonal planting material can also reduce the risk of fungal and viral diseases, substantially reducing production cost to under &#x0024;0.5 per gram to maintain a profitable cannabis production (\u003Ca href=\"#T4\">Table 4\u003C/a>). Considering these global demand scenarios, the supply of clean cannabis clones (pest free, and true to type tested) is an important supply chain component essential for the success and future growth of cannabis industry. To sustain and support the industry growth and make the production cost-effective, optimization in the cannabis tissue culture technology is vital.\u003C/p>\r\n\u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\">\u003C/div>\r\n\u003Cdiv class=\"Imageheaders\">TABLE 4\u003C/div>\r\n\u003Cdiv class=\"FigureDesc\">\r\n\u003Ca href=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/627240/fpls-12-627240-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-627240-t004.jpg\" name=\"table4\" target=\"_blank\">\r\n\n  \u003Cpicture>\n    \u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=480&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/627240/fpls-12-627240-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-627240-t004.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 563px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=370&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/627240/fpls-12-627240-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-627240-t004.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 1024px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=290&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/627240/fpls-12-627240-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-627240-t004.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 1441px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=410&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/627240/fpls-12-627240-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-627240-t004.jpg\" media=\"\">\u003Csource type=\"image/jpg\" srcset=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/627240/fpls-12-627240-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-627240-t004.jpg\" media=\"\"> \u003Cimg src=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/627240/fpls-12-627240-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-627240-t004.jpg\" alt=\"www.frontiersin.org\" id=\"T4\" loading=\"lazy\">\n  \u003C/picture>\n\u003C/a>\r\n\u003Cp>\u003Cstrong>Table 4.\u003C/strong> Comparison between tissue culture cloning and manual cloning in cannabis.\u003C/p>\r\n\u003C/div>\r\n\u003Cdiv class=\"clear\">\u003C/div>\r\n\u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\">\u003C/div>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb15 w100pc float_left mt15\">The \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> propagation of cannabis is superior to conventional methods because of disease-free elite plants&#x2019; production and a high multiplication rate. The cannabis industry is keen to invest in \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> propagation due to (i) saving footprint/production area by shifting a mother room to a tissue culture lab that will be almost 10% the size of the space needed same number of clones.\u003C/p>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb15\">The main hurdle of \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> propagation is the capital cost for the tissue culture lab setup. Setting up a massive large-scale production facility can involve a multimillion-dollar investment. Industry and technology will need to continue to improve and reduce costs so that \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> propagation can be affordable for all growers.\u003C/p>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb15\">In other plants, under a laminar flow hood setting, on an average of 100 plants per hour with 2000 working hours, 200,000 plants can be produced in a year. With an hourly labor cost of &#x0024;35 per hour will cost around &#x0024;0.35 per tissue culture plant (\u003Ca href=\"#B162\">Sluis, 2005\u003C/a>). This is around 60% of the production cost, adding another &#x0024;0.15 for other costs (including electricity, resources, and marketing) makes it a baseline cost of &#x0024;0.50 per plant. Scale also makes some impact on the cost of production being larger facilities can reduce the cost per plant significantly. These production costs can be as low as &#x0024;0.15 per plant if the plants are produced in India, Singapore, China, or Africa where labor costs are comparatively low.\u003C/p>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">A few biotech companies recently added robotic sub-culturing technology for their cannabis plantlets and developed a fully automated micropropagation system to reduce large-scale operation costs. However, the capital investment to purchase this kind of robotic system is incredibly high at this time. Automated technologies for media preparation and dispensing, photoautotrophic bioreactor systems, robotic explant handling, and cutting, transfer laser dissected explants into fresh culture media, and automated acclimatized and hardened plant packaging in future will make cannabis tissue culture industry high throughput and extremely cost-effective for assured &#x201C;\u003Ci>Just In Time\u003C/i>&#x201D; supply of pest free, true-to-type cannabis clones. A conceptual model for high throughput automated cannabis \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> clonal mass propagation is depicted in \u003Ca href=\"#F8\">Figure 8\u003C/a>. Robotics has the potential to bring tissue culture cost down by 25% (as low as &#x0024;0.15 per plant to compete with low-cost production in some parts of the world). Tissue culture automation technology is slowly progressing, and it will not only bring high-level consistent output but also reduce the cost of production as low as 20 cents per plant.\u003C/p>\r\n\u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\">\u003C/div>\r\n\u003Cdiv class=\"Imageheaders\">FIGURE 8\u003C/div>\r\n\u003Cdiv class=\"FigureDesc\">\r\n\u003Ca media=\"(max-width: 563px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=370&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/627240/fpls-12-627240-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-627240-g008.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: src=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/627240/fpls-12-627240-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-627240-g008.jpg\" alt=\"www.frontiersin.org\" id=\"F8\" loading=\"lazy\">\n  \u003C/picture>\n\u003C/a>\r\n\u003Cp>\u003Cstrong>Figure 8.\u003C/strong> Integration of automation and bioreactor technologies for mass propagation in cannabis for low cost clonal multiplication at \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> level.\u003C/p>\r\n\u003C/div>\r\n\u003Cdiv class=\"clear\">\u003C/div>\r\n\u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\">\u003C/div>\r\n\u003Ca id=\"S10\" name=\"S10\">\u003C/a>\u003Ch2>Conclusion\u003C/h2>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">The process of developing new varieties through conventional breeding can take 7&#x2013;12 years, depending on crop species. The progress of cannabis breeding programs is limited due to the difficulty in maintaining selected high yielding cross-pollinated elite genotypes under field or greenhouse conditions. Therefore, tissue culture techniques are advantageous for cannabis improvement because they can facilitate high multiplication rate and production of disease-free elite plants by overcoming the problems of heterozygosity from cross-pollination. The development of new industrial hemp and medical cannabis cultivars with improved traits could be further advanced using genome editing and other precision breeding tools, combined with \u003Ci>in vitro\u003C/i> techniques for regeneration. Unfortunately, hemp and cannabis plants&#x2019; dioecious nature complicates the efforts toward the improvement of specific traits, such as resistance to pests and diseases. Therefore, with the recent legalization, calls for serious targeted efforts are required to advance the regeneration and transformation protocols aiming to enhance the quality and safety of the plants and end products.\u003C/p>\r\n\u003Ca id=\"S11\" name=\"S11\">\u003C/a>\u003Ch2>Author Contributions\u003C/h2>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">All authors listed have made a substantial, direct and intellectual contribution to the work, and approved it for publication.\u003C/p>\r\n\u003Ca id=\"conf1\" name=\"conf1\">\u003C/a>\u003Ch2>Conflict of Interest\u003C/h2>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb15\">ME and RG were employed by the company Haplotech Inc.\u003C/p>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">The remaining authors declare that the research was conducted in the absence of any commercial or financial relationships that could be construed as a potential conflict of interest.\u003C/p>\r\n\u003Ca id=\"ack1\" name=\"ack1\">\u003C/a>\u003Ch2>Acknowledgments\u003C/h2>\r\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">We would like to thank Dr. Ayelign M.
